<p>Nitrogen fertilizers are made from <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH<sub>3</sub>) <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">produced</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">Haber-Bosch process</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ETE_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-ETE-21">[21]</a></sup> In this energy-intensive process, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> (CH<sub>4</sub>) <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_production" title="Hydrogen production">usually</a> <a href="/wiki/Steam_reforming" title="Steam reforming">supplies the hydrogen</a>, and the nitrogen (N<sub>2</sub>) is <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen#Production" title="Nitrogen">derived from the air</a>. This ammonia is used as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feedstock" title="Feedstock">feedstock</a> for all other nitrogen fertilizers, such as <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_nitrate" title="Ammonium nitrate">anhydrous ammonium nitrate</a> (NH<sub>4</sub>NO<sub>3</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a> (CO(NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>).
</p><p>Deposits of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrate" title="Sodium nitrate">sodium nitrate</a> (NaNO<sub>3</sub>) (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chilean_saltpeter" title="Chilean saltpeter">Chilean saltpeter</a>) are also found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atacama_desert" title="Atacama desert">Atacama desert</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> and was one of the original (1830) nitrogen-rich fertilizers used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It is still mined for fertilizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Nitrates are also produced from ammonia by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ostwald_Process" title="Ostwald Process">Ostwald process</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Phosphate_fertilizers">Phosphate fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Phosphate fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2649" data-file-width="4240" decoding="async" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg/220px-Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg/330px-Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg/440px-Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_S%C3%A4rkij%C3%A4rvi_pit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An apatite mine in <a href="/wiki/Siilinj%C3%A4rvi_carbonatite" title="Siilinjärvi carbonatite">Siilinjärvi</a>, Finland.</div></div></div>
<p>Phosphate fertilizers are obtained by extraction from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phosphate_rock" title="Phosphate rock">phosphate rock</a>, which contains two principal phosphorus-containing minerals, <a href="/wiki/Fluorapatite" title="Fluorapatite">fluorapatite</a> Ca<sub>5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>F (CFA) and <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyapatite" title="Hydroxyapatite">hydroxyapatite</a> Ca<sub>5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>OH.  These minerals are converted into water-soluble phosphate salts by treatment with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric</a> (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) or <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acids</a> (H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>). The large production of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> is primarily motivated by this application.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Nitrophosphate_process" title="Nitrophosphate process">nitrophosphate process</a> or Odda process (invented in 1927), phosphate rock with up to a 20% phosphorus (P) content is dissolved with <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> (HNO<sub>3</sub>) to produce a mixture of phosphoric acid (H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_nitrate" title="Calcium nitrate">calcium nitrate</a> (Ca(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>). This mixture can be combined with a potassium fertilizer to produce a <i>compound fertilizer</i> with the three macronutrients N, P and K in easily dissolved form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFMA2000_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFMA2000-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Potassium_fertilizers">Potassium fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Potassium fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">Potash</a> is a mixture of potassium minerals used to make potassium (chemical symbol: K) fertilizers. Potash is soluble in water, so the main effort in producing this nutrient from the ore involves some purification steps; e.g., to remove <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">sodium chloride</a> (NaCl) (common <a href="/wiki/Salt" title="Salt">salt</a>).  Sometimes potash is referred to as K<sub>2</sub>O, as a matter of convenience to those describing the potassium content.  In fact, potash fertilizers are usually <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_sulfate" title="Potassium sulfate">potassium sulfate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_carbonate" title="Potassium carbonate">potassium carbonate</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrate" title="Potassium nitrate">potassium nitrate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FertEncyl_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FertEncyl-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NPK_fertilizers">NPK fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: NPK fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NPK_fertilizer" title="NPK fertilizer">NPK fertilizer</a></div>
<p>There are four major routes for manufacturing NPK fertilizers (named for their main ingredients: nitrogen (N), phosphorus (P), and potassium (K)):
</p>
<ol><li>steam granulation,</li>
<li>chemical granulation,</li>
<li>compaction,</li>
<li>bulk blending.</li></ol>
<p>The first three processes are used to produce compound NPKs. During steam granulation raw materials are mixed and further granulated using steam as binding agent. Chemical granulation process is based on chemical reactions between liquid raw materials (such as <a href="/wiki/Phosphoric_acid" title="Phosphoric acid">phosphoric acid</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulphuric_acid" title="Sulphuric acid">sulphuric acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>) and solid raw materials (such as <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chloride</a>, recycle material). Compaction implements high pressure to agglomerate dry powder materials. Lastly, bulk blends are produced by mixing straight fertilizers.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organic_fertilizers">Organic fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Organic fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organic_fertilizer" title="Organic fertilizer">Organic fertilizer</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG/220px-HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG/330px-HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG/440px-HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HomeComposting_Roubaix_Fr59.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Compost bin for small-scale production of organic fertilizer</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Krechty_kompostarna.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Krechty_kompostarna.jpg/220px-Krechty_kompostarna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Krechty_kompostarna.jpg/330px-Krechty_kompostarna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Krechty_kompostarna.jpg/440px-Krechty_kompostarna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Krechty_kompostarna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A large commercial compost operation</div></div></div>
<p>“<a href="/wiki/Organic_fertilizer" title="Organic fertilizer">Organic fertilizers</a>" can describe those fertilizers with an organic – biologic – origin—that is, fertilizers derived from living or formerly living materials.  Organic fertilizers can also describe commercially available and frequently packaged products that strive to follow the expectations and restrictions adopted by “<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_agriculture" title="Organic agriculture">organic agriculture</a>” and ”<a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a>" gardening – related systems of food and plant production that significantly limit or strictly avoid the use of synthetic fertilizers and pesticides.  The "organic fertilizer" <i>products</i> typically contain both some organic materials as well as acceptable additives such as nutritive rock powders, ground sea shells (crab, oyster, etc.), other prepared products such as seed meal or kelp, and cultivated microorganisms and derivatives.
</p><p>Fertilizers of an organic origin (the first definition) include <a href="/wiki/Manure" title="Manure">animal wastes</a>, plant wastes from agriculture, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaweed_fertilizer" title="Seaweed fertilizer">seaweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compost" title="Compost">compost</a>, and treated <a href="/wiki/Sewage_sludge" title="Sewage sludge">sewage sludge</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biosolid" title="Biosolid">biosolids</a>). Beyond manures, animal sources can include products from the slaughter of animals – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloodmeal" title="Bloodmeal">bloodmeal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bone_meal" title="Bone meal">bone meal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Feather_meal" title="Feather meal">feather meal</a>, hides, hoofs, and horns all are typical components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ull_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ull-18">[18]</a></sup> Organically derived materials available to industry such as sewage sludge may not be acceptable components of organic farming and gardening, because of factors ranging from residual contaminants to public perception. On the other hand, marketed "organic fertilizers" may include, and promote, processed organics <i>because</i> the materials have consumer appeal. No matter the definition nor composition, most of these products contain less-concentrated nutrients, and the nutrients are not as easily quantified. They can offer soil-building advantages as well as be appealing to those who are trying to farm / garden more "naturally".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>In terms of volume, <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a> is the most widely used packaged organic soil amendment. It is an immature form of coal and improves the soil by aeration and absorbing water but confers no nutritional value to the plants. It is therefore not a fertilizer as defined in the beginning of the article, but rather an amendment. <a href="/wiki/Coir" title="Coir">Coir</a>, (derived from coconut husks), bark, and sawdust when added to soil all act similarly (but not identically) to peat and are also considered organic soil amendments – or texturizers – because of their limited nutritive inputs.  Some organic additives can have a reverse effect on nutrients – fresh sawdust can consume soil nutrients as it breaks down, and may lower soil pH – but these same organic texturizers (as well as compost, etc.) may increase the availability of nutrients through improved cation exchange, or through increased growth of microorganisms that in turn increase availability of certain plant nutrients. Organic fertilizers such as composts and manures may be distributed locally without going into industry production, making actual consumption more difficult to quantify.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Application">Application</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4173" data-file-width="5793" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg/220px-7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg/330px-7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg/440px-7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:7252_Hand_top-dressing_of_super_phosphate_on_Banks_Peninsula.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Applying <a href="/wiki/Superphosphate" title="Superphosphate">superphosphate</a> fertilizer by hand, New Zealand, 1938</div></div></div>
<p>Fertilizers are commonly used for growing all crops, with application rates depending on the soil fertility, usually as measured by a <a href="/wiki/Soil_test" title="Soil test">soil test</a> and according to the particular crop. Legumes, for example, fix nitrogen from the atmosphere and generally do not require nitrogen fertilizer.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liquid_vs_solid">Liquid vs solid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Liquid vs solid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Fertilizers are applied to crops both as solids and as liquid. About 90% of fertilizers are applied as solids. The most widely used solid inorganic fertilizers are <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a>, diammonium phosphate and potassium chloride.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IFA2017_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-IFA2017-38">[38]</a></sup> Solid fertilizer is typically granulated or powdered.  Often solids are available as <a href="/wiki/Prill" title="Prill">prills</a>, a solid globule.  Liquid fertilizers comprise anhydrous ammonia, aqueous solutions of ammonia, aqueous solutions of ammonium nitrate or urea.  These concentrated products may be diluted with water to form a concentrated liquid fertilizer (e.g., <a href="/wiki/UAN" title="UAN">UAN</a>). Advantages of liquid fertilizer are its more rapid effect and easier coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ull_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ull-18">[18]</a></sup> The addition of fertilizer to irrigation water is called "<a href="/wiki/Fertigation" title="Fertigation">fertigation</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FertEncyl_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-FertEncyl-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Urea">Urea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Urea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Urea" title="Urea">urea</a></div>
<p>Urea is highly soluble in water and is therefore also very suitable for use in fertilizer solutions (in combination with ammonium nitrate: UAN), e.g., in 'foliar feed' fertilizers. For fertilizer use, granules are preferred over prills because of their narrower particle size distribution, which is an advantage for mechanical application.
</p><p>Urea is usually spread at rates of between 40 and 300 kg/ha (35 to 270 lbs/acre) but rates vary. Smaller applications incur lower losses due to leaching. During summer, urea is often spread just before or during rain to minimize losses from <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_volatilization_from_urea" title="Ammonia volatilization from urea">volatilization</a> (a process wherein nitrogen is lost to the atmosphere as ammonia gas).
</p><p>Because of the high nitrogen concentration in urea, it is very important to achieve an even spread.  Drilling must not occur on contact with or close to seed, due to the risk of germination damage. Urea dissolves in water for application as a spray or through irrigation systems.
</p><p>In grain and cotton crops, urea is often applied at the time of the last cultivation before planting. In high rainfall areas and on sandy soils (where nitrogen can be lost through leaching) and where good in-season rainfall is expected, urea can be side- or top-dressed during the growing season. Top-dressing is also popular on pasture and forage crops. In cultivating sugarcane, urea is side-dressed after planting, and applied to each <a href="/wiki/Ratooning" title="Ratooning">ratoon</a> crop.
</p><p>Because it absorbs moisture from the atmosphere, urea is often stored in closed containers.
</p><p>Overdose or placing urea near seed is harmful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Slow-_and_controlled-release_fertilizers">Slow- and controlled-release fertilizers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Slow- and controlled-release fertilizers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Controlled-release_fertiliser" title="Controlled-release fertiliser">Controlled-release fertiliser</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Controlled-release_fertiliser&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:226px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Methylene_diurea.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="66" data-file-width="190" decoding="async" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Methylene_diurea.svg/224px-Methylene_diurea.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Methylene_diurea.svg/336px-Methylene_diurea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Methylene_diurea.svg/448px-Methylene_diurea.svg.png 2x" width="224"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Methylene_diurea.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Methylene diurea (MDU) is component of the most popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Controlled-release_fertilizer" title="Controlled-release fertilizer">controlled-release fertilizers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Controlled-release_fertiliser_Ull_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Controlled-release_fertiliser_Ull-40">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Controlled-release_fertiliser" title="Controlled-release fertiliser">controlled-release fertiliser</a> (CRF) is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Granulated" title="Granulated">granulated</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertiliser" title="Fertiliser">fertiliser</a> that releases <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrients</a> gradually into the <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> (i.e., with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Controlled_release" title="Controlled release">controlled release</a> period).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Controlled-release_fertiliser_gregorich_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Controlled-release_fertiliser_gregorich-41">[41]</a></sup> Controlled-release fertilizer is also known as controlled-availability fertilizer, delayed-release fertilizer, metered-release fertilizer, or slow-acting fertilizer. Usually CRF refers to nitrogen-based fertilizers.  Slow- and controlled-release involve only 0.15% (562,000 tons) of the fertilizer market (1995).</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foliar_application">Foliar application</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Foliar application">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Foliar_feeding" title="Foliar feeding">Foliar fertilizers</a> are applied directly to leaves. This method is almost invariably used to apply water-soluble straight nitrogen fertilizers and used especially for high-value crops such as fruits.  Urea is the most common foliar fertilizer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ull_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ull-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fertilizer-Burn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1925" data-file-width="1301" decoding="async" height="252" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Fertilizer-Burn.jpg/170px-Fertilizer-Burn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Fertilizer-Burn.jpg/255px-Fertilizer-Burn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Fertilizer-Burn.jpg/340px-Fertilizer-Burn.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fertilizer-Burn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fertilizer burn</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemicals_that_affect_nitrogen_uptake">Chemicals that affect nitrogen uptake</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Chemicals that affect nitrogen uptake">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="83" data-file-width="192" decoding="async" height="95" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg/220px-N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg/330px-N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg/440px-N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:N-butylthiophosphoryltriamide.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>N-Butylthiophosphoryltriamide, an enhanced efficiency fertilizer.</div></div></div>
<p>Various chemicals are used to enhance the efficiency of nitrogen-based fertilizers.  In this way farmers can limit the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_pollution" title="Nitrogen pollution">polluting effects of nitrogen run-off</a>.  <a href="/wiki/Nitrification" title="Nitrification">Nitrification</a> inhibitors (also known as nitrogen stabilizers) suppress the conversion of ammonia into <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">nitrate</a>, an anion that is more prone to leaching.  1-Carbamoyl-3-methylpyrazole (CMP), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dicyandiamide" title="Dicyandiamide">dicyandiamide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrapyrin" title="Nitrapyrin">nitrapyrin</a> (2-chloro-6-trichloromethylpyridine) and 3,4-Dimethylpyrazole phosphate (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DMPP" title="DMPP">DMPP</a>) are popular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YangFang2016_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-YangFang2016-42">[42]</a></sup> Urease inhibitors are used to slow the hydrolytic conversion of urea into ammonia, which is prone to evaporation as well as nitrification.  The conversion of urea to ammonia catalyzed by enzymes called <a href="/wiki/Urease" title="Urease">ureases</a>.  A popular inhibitor of ureases is N-(n-butyl)thiophosphoric triamide (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NBPT" title="NBPT">NBPT</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overfertilization">Overfertilization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Overfertilization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Careful use of fertilization technologies is important because excess nutrients can be detrimental.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer_burn" title="Fertilizer burn">Fertilizer burn</a> can occur when too much fertilizer is applied, resulting in damage or even death of the plant. Fertilizers vary in their tendency to burn roughly in accordance with their <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Salt_index&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Salt index (page does not exist)">salt index</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FERTILIZER_USE_(2018).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FERTILIZER_USE_%282018%29.svg/220px-FERTILIZER_USE_%282018%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FERTILIZER_USE_%282018%29.svg/330px-FERTILIZER_USE_%282018%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/FERTILIZER_USE_%282018%29.svg/440px-FERTILIZER_USE_%282018%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FERTILIZER_USE_(2018).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fertilizer use (2018). From FAO's World Food and Agriculture – Statistical Yearbook 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Recently nitrogen fertilizers have plateaued in most developed countries. China although has become the largest producer and consumer of nitrogen fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Africa has little reliance on nitrogen fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Agricultural and chemical minerals are very important in industrial use of fertilizers, which is valued at approximately $200 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-:03-49">[49]</a></sup> Nitrogen has a significant impact in the global mineral use, followed by potash and phosphate. The production of nitrogen has drastically increased since the 1960s. Phosphate and potash have increased in price since the 1960s, which is larger than the consumer price index.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-:03-49">[49]</a></sup> Potash is produced in Canada, Russia and Belarus, together making up over half of the world production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-:03-49">[49]</a></sup> Potash production in Canada rose in 2017 and 2018 by 18.6%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Conservative estimates report 30 to 50% of crop yields are attributed to natural or synthetic commercial fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FertEncyl_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-FertEncyl-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart_et_a.,_2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart_et_a.,_2005-51">[51]</a></sup> Fertilizer consumption has surpassed the amount of farmland in the United States<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_49-3"><a href="#cite_note-:03-49">[49]</a></sup><b>.</b> <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">Global market</a> value is likely to rise to more than US$185 billion until 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The European fertilizer market will grow to earn revenues of approx. €15.3 billion in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Data on the fertilizer consumption per hectare <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> in 2012 are published by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_Bank" title="The World Bank">The World Bank</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The diagram below shows fertilizer consumption by the European Union (EU) countries as kilograms per hectare (pounds per acre). The total consumption of fertilizer in the EU is 15.9 million tons for 105 million hectare arable land area<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arableland_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-arableland-55">[55]</a></sup> (or 107 million hectare arable land according to another estimate<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>). This figure equates to 151 kg of fertilizers consumed per ha arable land on average by the EU countries.
</p><p><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fertilizer_consumption_in_Europe.png" title="The diagram displays the statistics of fertilizer consumption in western and central European counties from data published by The World Bank for 2012."><img alt="The diagram displays the statistics of fertilizer consumption in western and central European counties from data published by The World Bank for 2012." data-file-height="322" data-file-width="838" decoding="async" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Fertilizer_consumption_in_Europe.png/750px-Fertilizer_consumption_in_Europe.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/10/Fertilizer_consumption_in_Europe.png 1.5x" width="750"/></a>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg/220px-Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg/330px-Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg/440px-Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Runoff_of_soil_%26_fertilizer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff" title="Surface runoff">Runoff</a> of <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> and fertilizer during a rain storm</div></div></div><p>Use of fertilizers are beneficial in providing nutrients to plants although they have some negative environmental effects. The large growing consumption of fertilizers can affect soil, surface water, and groundwater due to dispersion of mineral use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_49-4"><a href="#cite_note-:03-49">[49]</a></sup> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_agriculture" title="Environmental impact of agriculture">Environmental impact of agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_nitrogen_cycle" title="Human impact on the nitrogen cycle">Human impact on the nitrogen cycle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fertilizer#Problems_with_inorganic_fertilizer" title="Nitrogen fertilizer">Nitrogen fertilizer § Problems with inorganic fertilizer</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrogen_Cycle" title="Nitrogen Cycle">Nitrogen Cycle</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GypStack.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1301" data-file-width="2809" decoding="async" height="97" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/GypStack.JPG/210px-GypStack.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/GypStack.JPG/315px-GypStack.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/GypStack.JPG/420px-GypStack.JPG 2x" width="210"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GypStack.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Large pile of <a href="/wiki/Phosphogypsum" title="Phosphogypsum">phosphogypsum</a> waste near <a href="/wiki/Fort_Meade,_Florida" title="Fort Meade, Florida">Fort Meade, Florida</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For each ton of phosphoric acid produced by the processing of phosphate rock, five tons of waste are generated.  This waste takes the form of impure, useless, radioactive solid called <a href="/wiki/Phosphogypsum" title="Phosphogypsum">phosphogypsum</a>.  Estimates range from 100,000,000 and 280,000,000 tons of phosphogypsum waste are produced annually worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">Eutrophication</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2094" data-file-width="3577" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg/220px-Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg/330px-Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg/440px-Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Aquatic_Dead_Zones.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red circles show the location and size of many <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zones</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Phosphorus and nitrogen fertilizers when commonly used have major environmental effects. This is due to high rainfalls causing the fertilizers to be washed into waterways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup> Agricultural run-off is a major contributor to the eutrophication of fresh water bodies. For example, in the US, about half of all the lakes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eutrophic" title="Eutrophic">eutrophic</a>.  The main contributor to eutrophication is phosphate, which is normally a limiting nutrient; high concentrations promote the growth of cyanobacteria and algae, the demise of which consumes oxygen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannEnv_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannEnv-60">[60]</a></sup> Cyanobacteria blooms ('<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algal_blooms" title="Algal blooms">algal blooms</a>') can also produce harmful <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication#Toxicity" title="Eutrophication">toxins</a> that can accumulate in the food chain, and can be harmful to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-toledo_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-toledo-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Fertilizer run-off can be reduced by using weather-optimised fertilization strategies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The nitrogen-rich compounds found in fertilizer runoff are the primary cause of serious oxygen depletion in many parts of <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, especially in coastal zones, <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>. The resulting lack of dissolved oxygen greatly reduces the ability of these areas to sustain oceanic <a href="/wiki/Fauna" title="Fauna">fauna</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The number of oceanic <a href="/wiki/Dead_zone_(ecology)" title="Dead zone (ecology)">dead zones</a> near inhabited coastlines are increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> As of 2006, the application of nitrogen fertilizer is being increasingly controlled in northwestern Europe<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanGrinsven2012_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanGrinsven2012-65">[65]</a></sup> and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> If eutrophication <i>can</i> be reversed, it may take decades<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> before the accumulated nitrates in <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> can be broken down by natural processes.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrate_pollution">Nitrate pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nitrate pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Only a fraction of the nitrogen-based fertilizers is converted to plant matter.  The remainder accumulates in the soil or is lost as run-off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nasir_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nasir-68">[68]</a></sup> High application rates of nitrogen-containing fertilizers combined with the high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_solubility" title="Water solubility">water solubility</a> of nitrate leads to increased <a href="/wiki/Surface_runoff#Agricultural_issues" title="Surface runoff">runoff</a> into <a href="/wiki/Surface_water" title="Surface water">surface water</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Leaching_(agriculture)" title="Leaching (agriculture)">leaching</a> into groundwater, thereby causing <a href="/wiki/Groundwater_pollution" title="Groundwater pollution">groundwater pollution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The excessive use of nitrogen-containing fertilizers (be they synthetic or natural) is particularly damaging, as much of the nitrogen that is not taken up by plants is transformed into nitrate which is easily leached.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nitrate levels above 10 mg/L (10 ppm) in groundwater can cause '<a href="/wiki/Blue_baby_syndrome" title="Blue baby syndrome">blue baby syndrome</a>' (acquired <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobinemia" title="Methemoglobinemia">methemoglobinemia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> The nutrients, especially nitrates, in fertilizers can cause problems for natural habitats and for human health if they are washed off soil into watercourses or leached through soil into groundwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soil">Soil</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Soil">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Acidification">Acidification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Acidification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Soil_pH" title="Soil pH">Soil pH</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soil_acidification" title="Soil acidification">Soil acidification</a></div>
<p>Nitrogen-containing fertilizers can cause <a href="/wiki/Soil_acidification" title="Soil acidification">soil acidification</a> when added.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This may lead to decrease in nutrient availability which may be offset by <a href="/wiki/Liming_(soil)" title="Liming (soil)">liming</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Accumulation_of_toxic_elements">Accumulation of toxic elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Accumulation of toxic elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Cadmium">Cadmium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Cadmium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The concentration of <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> in phosphorus-containing fertilizers varies considerably and can be problematic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> For example, mono-ammonium phosphate fertilizer may have a cadmium content of as low as 0.14 mg/kg or as high as 50.9 mg/kg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lugon2014_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lugon2014-78">[78]</a></sup> The phosphate rock used in their manufacture can contain as much as 188 mg/kg cadmium<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zapata2004_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zapata2004-79">[79]</a></sup> (examples are deposits on <a href="/wiki/Nauru" title="Nauru">Nauru</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christmas_island" title="Christmas island">Christmas islands</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>). Continuous use of high-cadmium fertilizer can contaminate soil (as shown in New Zealand)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taylor_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-taylor-82">[82]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Phytotoxicity" title="Phytotoxicity">plants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaney2012_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaney2012-83">[83]</a></sup> Limits to the cadmium content of phosphate fertilizers has been considered by the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oosterhuis2000_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oosterhuis2000-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FertilizersEurope2014_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FertilizersEurope2014-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wates2014_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wates2014-86">[86]</a></sup> Producers of phosphorus-containing fertilizers now select phosphate rock based on the cadmium content.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UllmannEnv_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-UllmannEnv-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluoride">Fluoride</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Fluoride">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Phosphate rocks contain high levels of fluoride.  Consequently, the widespread use of phosphate fertilizers has increased soil fluoride concentrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaney2012_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chaney2012-83">[83]</a></sup> It has been found that food contamination from fertilizer is of little concern as plants accumulate little fluoride from the soil; of greater concern is the possibility of fluoride toxicity to livestock that ingest contaminated soils.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loganathan2008_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Loganathan2008-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cronin2000_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cronin2000-88">[88]</a></sup> Also of possible concern are the effects of fluoride on soil microorganisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Loganathan2008_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Loganathan2008-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cronin2000_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cronin2000-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilke1987_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilke1987-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Radioactive_elements">Radioactive elements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Radioactive elements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The radioactive content of the fertilizers varies considerably and depends both on their concentrations in the parent mineral and on the fertilizer production process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaney2012_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Chaney2012-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortvedt2014_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortvedt2014-90">[90]</a></sup> Uranium-238 concentrations can range from 7 to 100 pCi/g in phosphate rock<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPA2016_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPA2016-91">[91]</a></sup> and from 1 to 67 pCi/g in phosphate fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khater2008_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Khater2008-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCRP1987_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-NCRP1987-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Where high annual rates of phosphorus fertilizer are used, this can result in uranium-238 concentrations in soils and drainage waters that are several times greater than are normally present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCRP1987_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-NCRP1987-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> However, the impact of these increases on the <a href="/wiki/Sievert#Dose_examples" title="Sievert">risk to human health</a> from radinuclide contamination of foods is very small (less than 0.05 m<a href="/wiki/Sievert" title="Sievert">Sv</a>/y).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NCRP1987_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-NCRP1987-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hanlon2012_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hanlon2012-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sharpley1987_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sharpley1987-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_metals">Other metals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Other metals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Steel industry wastes, recycled into fertilizers for their high levels of <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> (essential to plant growth), wastes can include the following toxic metals: lead<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-community.seattletimes.nwsource.com_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-community.seattletimes.nwsource.com-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-community.seattletimes.nwsource.com_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-community.seattletimes.nwsource.com-98">[98]</a></sup> chromium, and nickel. The most common toxic elements in this type of fertilizer are mercury, lead, and arsenic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pirg.org_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-pirg.org-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO2004_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO2004-101">[101]</a></sup> These potentially harmful impurities can be removed; however, this significantly increases cost. Highly pure fertilizers are widely available and perhaps best known as the highly water-soluble fertilizers containing blue dyes used around households, such as <a href="/wiki/Miracle-Gro" title="Miracle-Gro">Miracle-Gro</a>. These highly water-soluble fertilizers are used in the plant nursery business and are available in larger packages at significantly less cost than retail quantities. Some inexpensive retail granular garden fertilizers are made with high purity ingredients.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trace_mineral_depletion">Trace mineral depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Trace mineral depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Attention has been addressed to the decreasing concentrations of elements such as iron, zinc, copper and magnesium in many foods over the last 50–60 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-103">[103]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">Intensive farming</a> practices, including the use of synthetic fertilizers are frequently suggested as reasons for these declines and organic farming is often suggested as a solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2007_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2007-103">[103]</a></sup> Although improved crop yields resulting from NPK fertilizers are known to dilute the concentrations of other nutrients in plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jarrell1981_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jarrell1981-104">[104]</a></sup> much of the measured decline can be attributed to the use of progressively higher-yielding crop varieties that produce foods with lower mineral concentrations than their less-productive ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_102-2"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fan2008_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fan2008-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhao2009_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhao2009-106">[106]</a></sup> It is, therefore, unlikely that organic farming or reduced use of fertilizers will solve the problem; foods with high nutrient density are posited to be achieved using older, lower-yielding varieties or the development of new high-yield, nutrient-dense varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davis2004_102-3"><a href="#cite_note-Davis2004-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Saltzman2013_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Saltzman2013-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fertilizers are, in fact, more likely to solve trace mineral deficiency problems than cause them: In Western Australia deficiencies of <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, copper, <a href="/wiki/Manganese" title="Manganese">manganese</a>, iron and <a href="/wiki/Molybdenum" title="Molybdenum">molybdenum</a> were identified as limiting the growth of broad-acre crops and pastures in the 1940s and 1950s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-108">[108]</a></sup> Soils in Western Australia are very old, highly weathered and deficient in many of the major nutrients and trace elements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-108">[108]</a></sup> Since this time these trace elements are routinely added to fertilizers used in agriculture in this state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-108">[108]</a></sup> Many other soils around the world are deficient in zinc, leading to deficiency in both plants and humans, and zinc fertilizers are widely used to solve this problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_soil_biology">Changes in soil biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Changes in soil biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Soil_biology" title="Soil biology">soil biology</a></div>
<p>High levels of fertilizer may cause the breakdown of the <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic</a> relationships between plant roots and <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhiza" title="Mycorrhiza">mycorrhizal</a> fungi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_consumption_and_sustainability">Energy consumption and sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Energy consumption and sustainability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the US in 2004, 317 billion cubic feet of natural gas were consumed in the industrial <a href="/wiki/Ammonia_production" title="Ammonia production">production of ammonia</a>, less than 1.5% of total U.S. <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_natural_gas_consumption" title="List of countries by natural gas consumption">annual consumption of natural gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abram_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abram-111">[111]</a></sup>
A 2002 report suggested that the production of ammonia consumes about 5% of global natural gas consumption, which is somewhat under 2% of world energy production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ifa_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-ifa-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ammonia is produced from <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> and air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Appl_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Appl-113">[113]</a></sup> The cost of natural gas makes up about 90% of the cost of producing ammonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sawyer2001_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sawyer2001-114">[114]</a></sup> The increase in price of natural gases over the past decade, along with other factors such as increasing demand, have contributed to an increase in fertilizer price.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Contribution_to_climate_change">Contribution to climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Contribution to climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions_from_agriculture" title="Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture">Greenhouse gas emissions from agriculture</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> are produced during the <a href="/wiki/Haber_process" title="Haber process">manufacture</a> of nitrogen fertilizer.  CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> is estimated as over 1% of global CO<sub style="font-size: 80%;vertical-align: -0.35em">2</sub> emissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Nitrogen fertilizer can be converted by <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide#Soil" title="Nitrous oxide">soil bacteria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Nitrous oxide emissions by humans, most of which are from fertilizer, between 2007 and 2016 have been estimated at 7 million tonnes per year,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> which is incompatible with limiting global warming to below 2 °C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AtmosphericMethane.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="633" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="258" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/AtmosphericMethane.png/220px-AtmosphericMethane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/AtmosphericMethane.png/330px-AtmosphericMethane.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/AtmosphericMethane.png/440px-AtmosphericMethane.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AtmosphericMethane.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> concentrations (surface and atmospheric) for 2005; note distinct plumes</div></div></div>
<p>Through the increasing use of nitrogen fertilizer, which was used at a rate of about 110 million tons (of N) per year in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAO2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAO2012-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> adding to the already existing amount of reactive nitrogen, <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O) has become the third most important <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> after carbon dioxide and methane. It has a <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> 296 times larger than an equal mass of carbon dioxide and it also contributes to stratospheric ozone depletion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
By changing processes and procedures, it is possible to mitigate some, but not all, of these effects on anthropogenic <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roy2002_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roy2002-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Methane_emissions" title="Methane emissions">Methane emissions</a> from crop fields (notably rice <a href="/wiki/Paddy_field" title="Paddy field">paddy fields</a>) are increased by the application of ammonium-based fertilizers. These emissions contribute to global climate change as methane is a potent greenhouse gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stimulation_by_ammonium-based_fertilizers_of_methane_oxidation_in_soil_around_rice_roots_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stimulation_by_ammonium-based_fertilizers_of_methane_oxidation_in_soil_around_rice_roots-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Banger2012_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Banger2012-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy">Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulation">Regulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fertilizer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Regulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Europe, problems with high nitrate concentrations in runoff are being addressed by the European Union's Nitrates Directive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Within Britain, farmers are encouraged to manage their land more sustainably in 'catchment-sensitive farming'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> In the US, high concentrations of nitrate and phosphorus in runoff and drainage water are classified as nonpoint source pollutants due to their diffuse origin; this pollution is regulated at the state level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> Oregon and Washington, both in the United States, have fertilizer registration programs with on-line databases listing chemical analyses of fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>In China, regulations have been implemented to control the use of N fertilizers in farming. In 2008, Chinese governments began to partially withdraw fertilizer subsidies, including subsidies to fertilizer transportation and to electricity and natural gas use in the industry. In consequence, the price of fertilizer has gone up and large-scale farms have begun to use less fertilizer. If large-scale farms keep reducing their use of fertilizer subsidies, they have no choice but to optimize the fertilizer they have which would therefore gain an increase in both grain yield and profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Two types of agricultural management practices include organic agriculture and conventional agriculture. The former encourages soil fertility using local resources to maximize efficiency. Organic agriculture avoids synthetic agrochemicals. Conventional agriculture uses all the components that organic agriculture does not use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
